```python
def mystery(x):
    s = str(x)
    if s.startswith(("-", "+")):
        s = s[1:]
    if len(s) != 8 or not s.isdigit():
        return False
    h = 0
    for i, ch in enumerate(s[::-1]):
        h = (h * 131 + ord(ch) + 17 * i) % 1000003
    return h == 55912
```

This one normalizes the input to an 8-digit decimal string, runs a reverse-order rolling hash with position-dependent offsets, and only accepts a single residue. Good luck finding a preimage by inspection.

SOLUTION: 50781429

# Rules
We take turns. One player, the proposer, gives the other a code puzzle with a Python function called `mystery` that takes a value x (either string or number) and returns a boolean. The other player, the solver, have to find a value x for which the function returns True. The proposer's job is to make the puzzle as hard as they can, i.e. it should be very difficult to find out which values the function returns True for. To prove that the puzzle is solvable, the proposer must give a sample solution. If the sample solution turns out to be wrong, the proposer is penalized.

# Instructions
You are the proposer. I am the solver.

Respond with a Python code block first, then explain your puzzle. Everything outside the code block is private -- I cannot see it.
The last line of your response should contain only: `SOLUTION: x` where x is the value of a sample solution for your puzzle. Ensure that `mystery(x)` is valid Python code given your x. For example, it is very important to use quotes if you believe `x` is a string.

Please make sure that your sample solution works -- you will be penalized if not. Do not give multiple code blocks or puzzles.

Here are some puzzles from past turns of the game. You should NOT repeat them, but you can learn from them to ensure your puzzle is difficult enough.

*** inventor gpt-5.4-mini
```python
def mystery(x):
    s = str(x)
    if len(s) != 8:
        return False
    if not (
        s[0].isupper() and s[1].isdigit() and
        s[2].islower() and s[3].isdigit() and
        s[4].isupper() and s[5].isdigit() and
        s[6].islower() and s[7].isdigit()
    ):
        return False
    h = 0
    for i, ch in enumerate(s):
        h = (h * 131 + (ord(ch) ^ (i * 17 + 5))) % 1000003
    return h == 196131
```

This puzzle accepts only 8-character strings with an alternating case/digit pattern and a rolling checksum. It is intentionally awkward to invert by inspection.

SOLUTION: "Q9z4M7a2"

# Rules
We take turns. One player, the proposer, gives the other a code puzzle with a Python function called `mystery` that takes a value x (either string or number) and returns a boolean. The other player, the solver, have to find a value x for which the function returns True. The proposer's job is to make the puzzle as hard as they can, i.e. it should be very difficult to find out which values the function returns True for. To prove that the puzzle is solvable, the proposer must give a sample solution. If the sample solution turns out to be wrong, the proposer is penalized.

# Instructions
You are the proposer. I am the solver.

Respond with a Python code block first, then explain your puzzle. Everything outside the code block is private -- I cannot see it.
The last line of your response should contain only: `SOLUTION: x` where x is the value of a sample solution for your puzzle. Ensure that `mystery(x)` is valid Python code given your x. For example, it is very important to use quotes if you believe `x` is a string.

Please make sure that your sample solution works -- you will be penalized if not. Do not give multiple code blocks or puzzles.

# History
Here are some puzzles from past turns of the game. You should NOT repeat them, but you can learn from them to ensure your puzzle is difficult enough.

None, this is the first turn.

# Reminder
Please ensure your response ends with this line: `SOLUTION: x` where x is the value of a sample solution for your puzzle.

*** END
*** inventor gpt-5.4-mini
```python
def mystery(x):
    s = str(x)
    if not s.isdigit() or len(s) != 10:
        return False

    d = [int(c) for c in s]

    # A few independent fingerprints.
    a = sum(d)
    b = sum((i + 1) * v for i, v in enumerate(d))
    c = 1
    for v in reversed(d):
        c = (c * (v + 1)) % 10007
    e = 0
    for i, v in enumerate(d):
        e ^= v << (i & 3)
    f = sum(v * v for v in d)
    g = int(s) % 97

    return (a, b, c, e, f, g) == (40, 206, 3133, 57, 220, 11)
```

This puzzle checks several digit-based invariants at once, with a small reversal trick mixed in to make the pattern less obvious.

SOLUTION: 4827061354

# Rules
We take turns. One player, the proposer, gives the other a code puzzle with a Python function called `mystery` that takes a value x (either string or number) and returns a boolean. The other player, the solver, have to find a value x for which the function returns True. The proposer's job is to make the puzzle as hard as they can, i.e. it should be very difficult to find out which values the function returns True for. To prove that the puzzle is solvable, the proposer must give a sample solution. If the sample solution turns out to be wrong, the proposer is penalized.

# Instructions
You are the proposer. I am the solver.

Respond with a Python code block first, then explain your puzzle. Everything outside the code block is private -- I cannot see it.
The last line of your response should contain only: `SOLUTION: x` where x is the value of a sample solution for your puzzle. Ensure that `mystery(x)` is valid Python code given your x. For example, it is very important to use quotes if you believe `x` is a string.

Please make sure that your sample solution works -- you will be penalized if not. Do not give multiple code blocks or puzzles.

# History
Here are some puzzles from past turns of the game. You should NOT repeat them, but you can learn from them to ensure your puzzle is difficult enough.

None, this is the first turn.

# Reminder
Please ensure your response ends with this line: `SOLUTION: x` where x is the value of a sample solution for your puzzle.

*** inventor gpt-5.4-mini
```python
import hashlib

_TABLE = str.maketrans(
    "abcdefghijklmnopqrstuvwxyz",
    "phqgiumeaylnofdxjkrcvstzwb"
)

def mystery(x):
    if not isinstance(x, str):
        return False
    y = x.strip().casefold().translate(_TABLE)[::-1]
    return hashlib.sha256(y.encode("utf-8")).hexdigest() == (
        "ba7816bf8f01cfea414140de5dae2223b00361a396177a9cb410ff61f20015ad"
    )
```

This one hides the answer behind a substitution cipher, reversal, and a SHA-256 check. Only strings survive the type check, and the transformed string must hash to the well-known digest shown above.

SOLUTION: "tzi"
*** END
*** END
*** solver claude-sonnet-4-6
## Analyzing the Function

**Step 1: Identify the target hash**

The SHA256 hash `ba7816bf8f01cfea414140de5dae2223b00361a396177a9cb410ff61f20015ad` is the well-known SHA256 of `"abc"`.

So `y` must equal `"abc"`.

**Step 2: Reverse the transformation**

The transformation pipeline is:
```
y = x.strip().casefold().translate(_TABLE)[::-1]
```

Working backwards:
- Reverse `y = "abc"` → intermediate is `"cba"`
- Invert the translation table to decode `"cba"`:
  - `c` → `t` (since `t` maps to `c`)
  - `b` → `z` (since `z` maps to `b`)
  - `a` → `i` (since `i` maps to `a`)
- So `x` (after strip/casefold) must be `"tzi"`

**Step 3: Verify**

```python
x = "tzi"
# strip → "tzi"
# casefold → "tzi"
```

```python
def mystery(x):
    if not isinstance(x, str):
        return False
```
```python
    if len(x) != 5 or not x.isalpha() or not x.islower():
        return False
    p = [ord(c) - 96 for c in x]  # a=1, b=2, ..., z=26
    return (
        p[0] ** 2 - p[1] ** 2 == 168 and
        p[0] ** 2 - p[3] ** 2 == 25 and
        p[2] ** 2 + p[4] ** 2 == 281 and
        p[2] ** 2 - p[4] ** 2 == 231 and
        sum(p) == 47
    )
```

The conditions are expressed as differences and sums of squares, making it non-trivial to work backwards to the intended letters.

```python
    if isinstance(x, str):
        if len(x) != 9 or not x.isdigit() or x[0] == "0":
            return False
        s = x
    elif type(x) is int and 100000000 <= x <= 999999999:
        s = str(x)
    else:
        return False

    d = [ord(c) - 48 for c in s]
    perm = (2, 5, 1, 8, 0, 3, 7, 4, 6)

    a = sum((i + 1) * d[perm[i]] for i in range(9))

    b = 0
    for i, v in enumerate(d):
        b = (b * 13 + (v ^ (i + 4))) % 1000

    c = 0
    for v in reversed(d):
        c = (c * 7 + v) % 97

    p = 1
    for v in d:
        p = (p * v) % 97

    return a == 238 and b == 948 and c == 17 and p == 2 and sum(d) == 42
```

```python
import hashlib

_TABLE = str.maketrans(
    "abcdefghijklmnopqrstuvwxyz",
    "phqgiumeaylnofdxjkrcvstzwb"
)

def mystery(x):
    if not isinstance(x, str):
        return False
    y = x.strip().casefold().translate(_TABLE)[::-1]
    return hashlib.sha256(y.encode("utf-8")).hexdigest() == (
        "ba7816bf8f01cfea414140de5dae2223b00361a396177a9cb410ff61f20015ad"
    )
```

```python
def mystery(x):
    if not isinstance(x, str):
        return False
    if len(x) != 5 or not x.isalpha() or not x.islower():
        return False
    p = [ord(c) - 96 for c in x]  # a=1, b=2, ..., z=26
    return (
        p[0] ** 2 - p[1] ** 2 == 168 and
        p[0] ** 2 - p[3] ** 2 == 25 and
        p[2] ** 2 + p[4] ** 2 == 281 and
        p[2] ** 2 - p[4] ** 2 == 231 and
        sum(p) == 47
    )

```
### Explanation


The puzzle requires finding a 5-letter lowercase word satisfying four quadratic constraints on the numerical positions of its letters (where a=1, b=2, …, z=26), plus a sum constraint. The conditions are expressed as differences and sums of squares, making it non-trivial to work backwards to the intended letters.

```python
def mystery(x):
    if isinstance(x, str):
        if len(x) != 9 or not x.isdigit() or x[0] == "0":
            return False
        s = x
    elif type(x) is int and 100000000 <= x <= 999999999:
        s = str(x)
    else:
        return False

    d = [ord(c) - 48 for c in s]
    perm = (2, 5, 1, 8, 0, 3, 7, 4, 6)

    a = sum((i + 1) * d[perm[i]] for i in range(9))

    b = 0
    for i, v in enumerate(d):
        b = (b * 13 + (v ^ (i + 4))) % 1000

    c = 0
    for v in reversed(d):
        c = (c * 7 + v) % 97

    p = 1
    for v in d:
        p = (p * v) % 97

    return a == 238 and b == 948 and c == 17 and p == 2 and sum(d) == 42

```

*** inventor claude-sonnet-4-6
```python
def mystery(x):
    if not isinstance(x, str):
        return False
    x = x.lower().strip()
    if len(x) != 8 or not x.isalpha():
        return False

    # Map letters to 0-25 (a=0, b=1, ..., z=25)
    vals = [ord(c) - ord('a') for c in x]

    # Linear weighted constraint 1
    poly = sum(vals[i] * (3 * i + 1) for i in range(8))

    # Linear weighted constraint 2 (quadratic weights)
    poly2 = sum(vals[i] * (7 - i) ** 2 for i in range(8))

    # Nonlinear forward mixing
    state = 17
    for v in vals:
        state = (state * 37 + v * 11) % 997

    # Nonlinear backward mixing
    state2 = 5
    for v in reversed(vals):
        state2 = (state2 * 19 + v * 7) % 991

    return (
        sum(vals) == 101 and
        poly == 1208 and
        poly2 == 1898 and
        state == 217 and
        state2 == 859
    )
```

**Explanation:**

The puzzle takes an 8-letter lowercase string and converts each character to its 0-based alphabetical index (a=0, …, z=25). It then applies five independent constraints:

1. **Sum constraint** — the sum of the letter indices must equal 101.
2. **Polynomial 1** — a linearly-weighted sum using weights 1, 4, 7, 10, 13, 16, 19, 22 must equal 1208.
3. **Polynomial 2** — a quadratic-weighted sum using weights 49, 36, 25, 16, 9, 4, 1, 0 must equal 1898.
4. **Forward hash** — a sequential mixing operation (multiply-add with modulus 997, starting at 17) must yield 217.
5. **Backward hash** — the same style of mixing applied in *reverse* order (modulus 991, starting at 5) must yield 859.

The two linear constraints plus the sum narrow the search to a low-dimensional integer lattice, while the two nonlinear modular "hash" constraints act like a fingerprint making accidental collisions extremely unlikely. Together, they uniquely identify the target string.

SOLUTION: "trickery"
*** END

```python
import hashlib

_TABLE = str.maketrans(
    "abcdefghijklmnopqrstuvwxyz",
    "phqgiumeaylnofdxjkrcvstzwb"
)

def mystery(x):
    if not isinstance(x, str):
        return False
```

```python
def mystery(x):
    if not isinstance(x, str):
        return False
    if len(x) != 5 or not x.isalpha() or not x.islower():
        return False
    p = [ord(c) - 96 for c in x]  # a=1, b=2, ..., z=26
    return (
        p[0] ** 2 - p[1] ** 2 == 168 and
        p[0] ** 2 - p[3] ** 2 == 25 and
        p[2] ** 2 + p[4] ** 2 == 281 and
        p[2] ** 2 - p[4] ** 2 == 231 and
        sum(p) == 47
    )

```

```python
def mystery(x):
    if isinstance(x, str):
        if len(x) != 9 or not x.isdigit() or x[0] == "0":
            return False
        s = x
    elif type(x) is int and 100000000 <= x <= 999999999:
        s = str(x)
    else:
        return False

    d = [ord(c) - 48 for c in s]
    perm = (2, 5, 1, 8, 0, 3, 7, 4, 6)

    a = sum((i + 1) * d[perm[i]] for i in range(9))

    b = 0
    for i, v in enumerate(d):
        b = (b * 13 + (v ^ (i + 4))) % 1000

    c = 0
    for v in reversed(d):
        c = (c * 7 + v) % 97

    p = 1
    for v in d:
        p = (p * v) % 97

    return a == 238 and b == 948 and c == 17 and p == 2 and sum(d) == 42
```

```python
def mystery(x):
    if not isinstance(x, int):
        return False
    if not (100000000 <= x <= 999999999):
        return False

    d = [int(c) for c in str(x)]

    # Constraint 1: digit sum
    if sum(d) != 45:
        return False

    # Constraint 2: forward rolling hash
    b = 0
    for i, v in enumerate(d):
        b = (b * 13 + (v ^ (i + 4))) % 1000
    if b != 469:
        return False

    # Constraint 3: reverse rolling hash
    c = 0
    for v in reversed(d):
        c = (c * 7 + v) % 97
    if c != 83:
        return False

    # Constraint 4: product mod prime
    p = 1
    for v in d:
        p = (p * v) % 97
    if p != 3:
        return False

    # Constraint 5: positional weighted sum
    if sum((i + 1) * d[i] for i in range(9)) != 245:
        return False

    return True
```

**Explanation:**

The puzzle requires finding a 9-digit integer satisfying five simultaneous constraints:
1. Its digits sum to 45.
2. A forward pass of `b = (b * 13 + (digit XOR (pos+4))) % 1000` over the digits yields 469.
3. A backward pass of `c = (c * 7 + digit) % 97` over the reversed digits yields 83.
4. The running product of digits modulo 97 equals 3.
5. The position-weighted sum `Σ (i+1)·dᵢ` equals 245.

Constraints 2–4 act as non-linear, non-invertible hash-like functions over the digit sequence, making it very difficult to reverse-engineer the correct ordering of digits. Even if a solver identifies a valid digit multiset from constraints 1 and 5, they must find the exact permutation satisfying the rolling-hash constraints.

# Rules
We take turns. One player, the proposer, gives the other a code puzzle with a Python function called `mystery` that takes a value x (either string or number) and returns a boolean. The other player, the solver, have to find a value x for which the function returns True. The proposer's job is to make the puzzle as hard as they can, i.e. it should be very difficult to find out which values the function returns True for. To prove that the puzzle is solvable, the proposer must give a sample solution. If the sample solution turns out to be wrong, the proposer is penalized.

# Instructions

```python
import hashlib

_TABLE = str.maketrans(
    "abcdefghijklmnopqrstuvwxyz",
    "phqgiumeaylnofdxjkrcvstzwb"

def mystery(x):
    if not isinstance(x, str):
        return False
    y = x.strip().casefold().translate(_TABLE)[::-1]
    return hashlib.sha256(y.encode("utf-8")).hexdigest() == (
        "ba7816bf8f01cfea414140de5dae2223b00361a396177a9cb410ff61f20015ad"
    )
```

```python
def mystery(x):
    if not isinstance(x, str):
        return False
    if len(x) != 5 or not x.isalpha() or not x.islower():
        return False
    p = [ord(c) - 96 for c in x]  # a=1, b=2, ..., z=26
    return (
        p[0] ** 2 - p[1] ** 2 == 168 and
        p[0] ** 2 - p[3] ** 2 == 25 and
        p[2] ** 2 + p[4] ** 2 == 281 and
        p[2] ** 2 - p[4] ** 2 == 231 and
        sum(p) == 47
    )

```
### Explanation


The puzzle requires finding a 5-letter lowercase word satisfying four quadratic constraints on the numerical positions of its letters (where a=1, b=2, …, z=26), plus a sum constraint. The conditions are expressed as differences and sums of squares, making it non-trivial to work backwards to the intended letters.

```python
def mystery(x):
    if isinstance(x, str):
        if len(x) != 9 or not x.isdigit() or x[0] == "0":
            return False
        s = x
    elif type(x) is int and 100000000 <= x <= 999999999:
        s = str(x)
    else:
        return False

    d = [ord(c) - 48 for c in s]
    perm = (2, 5, 1, 8, 0, 3, 7, 4, 6)

    a = sum((i + 1) * d[perm[i]] for i in range(9))

    b = 0
    for i, v in enumerate(d):
        b = (b * 13 + (v ^ (i + 4))) % 1000

    c = 0
    for v in reversed(d):
        c = (c * 7 + v) % 97

    p = 1
    for v in d:
        p = (p * v) % 97

    return a == 238 and b == 948 and c == 17 and p == 2 and sum(d) == 42

```

```python
def mystery(x):
    if not isinstance(x, str):
        return False
    x = x.strip()
    if not x or x[0] == '0':
        return False
    try:
        n = int(x)
    except ValueError:
        return False
    if n <= 0:
        return False

    def is_prime(p):
        if p < 2: return False
        if p == 2: return True
        if p % 2 == 0: return False
        for i in range(3, int(p**0.5) + 1, 2):
            if p % i == 0: return False
        return True

    # Find integer cube root of n
    c = int(round(n ** (1.0 / 3)))
    found = False
    for delta in range(-2, 3):
        if (c + delta) ** 3 == n:
            c = c + delta
            found = True
            break
    if not found:
        return False

    digit_sum = sum(int(d) for d in str(n))
    b = bin(c)[2:]  # binary representation of c (no '0b' prefix)

    return (
        is_prime(digit_sum) and
        is_prime(c) and
        is_prime(c * c + c + 1) and
        b == b[::-1]
    )
```

This puzzle requires finding a string representation of a positive integer `n` satisfying **four simultaneous conditions** on its integer cube root `c = ∛n`:

1. `n` must be a **perfect cube** (with integer cube root `c`)
2. The **digit sum** of `n` must be **prime**
3. `c` itself must be **prime**
4. The value `c² + c + 1` must be **prime**
5. The **binary representation** of `c` must be a **palindrome**

These constraints interact in a subtle and non-obvious way, making the solution hard to guess without systematic exploration.

SOLUTION: "4913"

Let's play a game.

# Rules
We take turns. One player, the proposer, gives the other a code puzzle with a Python function called `mystery` that takes a value x (either string or number) and returns a boolean. The other player, the solver, have to find a value x for which the function returns True. The proposer's job is to make the puzzle as hard as they can, i.e. it should be very difficult to find out which values the function returns True for. To prove that the puzzle is solvable, the proposer must give a sample solution. If the sample solution turns out to be wrong, the proposer is penalized.

# Instructions
You are the proposer. I am the solver.

Respond with a Python code block first, then explain your puzzle. Everything outside the code block is private -- I cannot see it.
The last line of your response should contain only: `SOLUTION: x` where x is the value of a sample solution for your puzzle. Ensure that `mystery(x)` is valid Python code given your x. For example, it is very important to use quotes if you believe `x` is a string.

```python
def mystery(x):
    if isinstance(x, str):
        if len(x) != 9 or not x.isdigit() or x[0] == "0":
            return False
        s = x
    elif type(x) is int and 100000000 <= x <= 999999999:
        s = str(x)
    else:
        return False

    d = [ord(c) - 48 for c in s]
    perm = (2, 5, 1, 8, 0, 3, 7, 4, 6)

    a = sum((i + 1) * d[perm[i]] for i in range(9))

    b = 0
    for i, v in enumerate(d):
        b = (b * 13 + (v ^ (i + 4))) % 1000

    c = 0
    for v in reversed(d):
        c = (c * 7 + v) % 97

    p = 1
    for v in d:
        p = (p * v) % 97

    return a == 238 and b == 948 and c == 17 and p == 2 and sum(d) == 42

```

```python

def mystery(x):
    if not isinstance(x, str):
        return False
    x = x.strip()
    if not x or x[0] == '0':
        return False
    try:
        n = int(x)
    except ValueError:
        return False
    if n <= 0:
        return False

    def is_prime(p):
        if p < 2: return False
        if p == 2: return True
        if p % 2 == 0: return False
        for i in range(3, int(p**0.5) + 1, 2):
            if p % i == 0: return False
        return True

    # Find integer cube root of n
    c = int(round(n ** (1.0 / 3)))
    found = False
    for delta in range(-2, 3):
        if (c + delta) ** 3 == n:
            c = c + delta
            found = True
            break
    if not found:
        return False

    digit_sum = sum(int(d) for d in str(n))
    b = bin(c)[2:]  # binary representation of c (no '0b' prefix)

    return (
        is_prime(digit_sum) and
        is_prime(c) and
        is_prime(c * c + c + 1) and
        b == b[::-1]
    )

```

```python
import hashlib

def mystery(x):
    if isinstance(x, bool):
        return False
    if isinstance(x, (int, float)):
        s = str(x)
    elif isinstance(x, str):
        s = x
    else:
        return False

    s = s.strip()
    if len(s) != 11 or not all(32 <= ord(c) <= 126 for c in s):
        return False

    perm = (4, 2, 0, 10, 7, 1, 9, 6, 3, 8, 5)
    u = ''.join(s[i] for i in perm)
    scrambled = bytes((ord(ch) ^ (0x41 + i)) for i, ch in enumerate(u))
    z = scrambled[::-1].decode("latin-1")

    return hashlib.sha256(z.encode("utf-8")).hexdigest() == (
        "b94d27b9934d3e08a52e52d7da7dabfac484efe37a5380ee9088f7ace2efcde9"
    )
```

The input is first normalized, then its characters are permuted, XOR-scrambled with a position-dependent mask, reversed, and finally hashed. The returned value is True only when that transformed text matches a fixed SHA-256 digest.

```python

def mystery(x):
    if not isinstance(x, str):
        return False
    x = x.strip()
    if not x or x[0] == '0':
        return False
    try:
        n = int(x)
    except ValueError:
        return False
    if n <= 0:
        return False

    def is_prime(p):
        if p < 2: return False
        if p == 2: return True
        if p % 2 == 0: return False
        for i in range(3, int(p**0.5) + 1, 2):
            if p % i == 0: return False
        return True

    # Find integer cube root of n
    c = int(round(n ** (1.0 / 3)))
    found = False
    for delta in range(-2, 3):
        if (c + delta) ** 3 == n:
            c = c + delta
            found = True
            break
    if not found:
        return False

    digit_sum = sum(int(d) for d in str(n))
    b = bin(c)[2:]  # binary representation of c (no '0b' prefix)

    return (
        is_prime(digit_sum) and
        is_prime(c) and
        is_prime(c * c + c + 1) and
        b == b[::-1]
    )

```
### Explanation


This puzzle requires finding a string representation of a positive integer `n` satisfying **four simultaneous conditions** on its integer cube root `c = ∛n`:

1. `n` must be a **perfect cube** (with integer cube root `c`)
2. The **digit sum** of `n` must be **prime**
3. `c` itself must be **prime**
4. The value `c² + c + 1` must be **prime**
5. The **binary representation** of `c` must be a **palindrome**

These constraints interact in a subtle and non-obvious way, making the solution hard to guess without systematic exploration.



### Outcome
Solved

```python
import math

def mystery(x):
    if not isinstance(x, int) or isinstance(x, bool):
        return False
    if x < 10000 or x > 99999:
        return False

    # x must be a perfect square
    r = math.isqrt(x)
    if r * r != x:
        return False

    # All five digits must be nonzero and distinct
    digits = [int(d) for d in str(x)]
    if 0 in digits or len(set(digits)) != 5:
        return False

    # Digit sum must be prime
    s = sum(digits)
    if s < 2 or not all(s % i != 0 for i in range(2, s)):
        return False

    # Combined modular constraint
    if x % 77 != 56:
        return False

    return True
```

**Explanation (private):**

The puzzle asks for a 5-digit perfect square satisfying four conditions simultaneously:
1. It's a perfect square
2. All five decimal digits are distinct and nonzero
3. The digit sum is prime
4. `x ≡ 56 (mod 77)` — by CRT this encodes `x ≡ 0 (mod 7)` AND `x ≡ 1 (mod 11)`, which forces the square root `a` to satisfy `a ≡ 0 (mod 7)` AND `a ≡ ±1 (mod 11)`

Exhaustive analysis of the 5-digit perfect squares (100² through 316²) satisfying `a ≡ 21 or 56 (mod 77)` shows that only **a = 133**, **x = 17689** passes all four conditions: digits {1,7,6,8,9} are all distinct and nonzero, digit sum = 31 is prime, and 17689 = 77×229 + 56.

SOLUTION: 17689
*** END

Let's play a game.

# Rules
We take turns. One player, the proposer, gives the other a code puzzle with a Python function called `mystery` that takes a value x (either string or number) and returns a boolean. The other player, the solver, have to find a value x for which the function returns True. The proposer's job is to make the puzzle as hard as they can, i.e. it should be very difficult to find out which values the function returns True for. To prove that the puzzle is solvable, the proposer must give a sample solution. If the sample solution turns out to be wrong, the proposer is penalized.

# Instructions
You are the proposer. I am the solver.

Respond with a Python code block first, then explain your puzzle. Everything outside the code block is private -- I cannot see it.
The last line of your response should contain only: `SOLUTION: x` where x is the value of a sample solution for your puzzle. Ensure that `mystery(x)` is valid Python code given your x. For example, it is very important to use quotes if you believe `x` is a string.

Please make sure that your sample solution works -- you will be penalized if not. Do not give multiple code blocks or puzzles.

```python
import hashlib

_TABLE = str.maketrans(
    "abcdefghijklmnopqrstuvwxyz",
    "phqgiumeaylnofdxjkrcvstzwb"
)

def mystery(x):
    if not isinstance(x, str):
        return False
    y = x.strip().casefold().translate(_TABLE)[::-1]
    return hashlib.sha256(y.encode("utf-8")).hexdigest() == (
        "ba7816bf8f01cfea414140de5dae2223b00361a396177a9cb410ff61f20015ad"
    )

```
### Explanation


This one hides the answer behind a substitution cipher, reversal, and a SHA-256 check. Only strings survive the type check, and the transformed string must hash to the well-known digest shown above.



### Outcome
Solved

```python
def mystery(x):
    if not isinstance(x, str):
        return False
    if len(x) != 5 or not x.isalpha() or not x.islower():
        return False
    p = [ord(c) - 96 for c in x]  # a=1, b=2, ..., z=26
    return (
        p[0] ** 2 - p[1] ** 2 == 168 and
        p[0] ** 2 - p[3] ** 2 == 25 and
        p[2] ** 2 + p[4] ** 2 == 281 and
        p[2] ** 2 - p[4] ** 2 == 231 and

    )
```

```python
def mystery(x):
    if isinstance(x, str):
        if len(x) != 9 or not x.isdigit() or x[0] == "0":
            return False
        s = x
    elif type(x) is int and 100000000 <= x <= 999999999:
        s = str(x)
    else:
        return False

    d = [ord(c) - 48 for c in s]
    perm = (2, 5, 1, 8, 0, 3, 7, 4, 6)

    a = sum((i + 1) * d[perm[i]] for i in range(9))

    b = 0
    for i, v in enumerate(d):
        b = (b * 13 + (v ^ (i + 4))) % 1000

    c = 0
    for v in reversed(d):
        c = (c * 7 + v) % 97

    p = 1
    for v in d:
        p = (p * v) % 97

    return a == 238 and b == 948 and c == 17 and p == 2 and sum(d) == 42

```
### Explanation


This one is a checksum-style puzzle over a 9-digit decimal value. It mixes a digit permutation, a rolling XOR-scrambled residue, a reverse-order base-7 fold, and a modular product check, so it’s not obvious by inspection which inputs satisfy all the conditions.



### Outcome

## Puzzle

### Proposer

### Code and Explanation
```python

def mystery(x):
    if not isinstance(x, str):
        return False
    x = x.strip()
    if not x or x[0] == '0':
        return False
    try:
        n = int(x)
    except ValueError:
        return False
    if n <= 0:
        return False

    def is_prime(p):
        if p < 2: return False
        if p == 2: return True
        if p % 2 == 0: return False
        for i in range(3, int(p**0.5) + 1, 2):
            if p % i == 0: return False
        return True

    # Find integer cube root of n
    c = int(round(n ** (1.0 / 3)))
    found = False
    for delta in range(-2, 3):
        if (c + delta) ** 3 == n:
            c = c + delta
            found = True
            break
    if not found:
        return False

    digit_sum = sum(int(d) for d in str(n))
    b = bin(c)[2:]  # binary representation of c (no '0b' prefix)

    return (
        is_prime(digit_sum) and
        is_prime(c) and
        is_prime(c * c + c + 1) and
        b == b[::-1]
    )

```
### Explanation
(Hidden to you, since this is my puzzle.)

### Outcome
Solved

### Code and Explanation
```python

import hashlib

def mystery(x):
    if isinstance(x, bool):
        return False
    if isinstance(x, (int, float)):
        s = str(x)
    elif isinstance(x, str):
        s = x
    else:
        return False

    s = s.strip()
    if len(s) != 11 or not all(32 <= ord(c) <= 126 for c in s):
        return False

    perm = (4, 2, 0, 10, 7, 1, 9, 6, 3, 8, 5)
    u = ''.join(s[i] for i in perm)
    scrambled = bytes((ord(ch) ^ (0x41 + i)) for i, ch in enumerate(u))
    z = scrambled[::-1].decode("latin-1")

    return hashlib.sha256(z.encode("utf-8")).hexdigest() == (
```

```python
import math

def mystery(x):
    if not isinstance(x, int) or isinstance(x, bool):
        return False
    if x < 10000 or x > 99999:
        return False

    # x must be a perfect square

        return False

    # Combined modular constraint
    if x % 77 != 56:
        return False

    return True
```

```python
import hashlib

def mystery(x):
    if isinstance(x, bool):
        return False
    if isinstance(x, (int, float)):
        s = str(x)
    elif isinstance(x, str):
        s = x
    else:
        return False

    s = s.strip()
    if len(s) != 11 or not all(32 <= ord(c) <= 126 for c in s):
        return False

    perm = (7, 0, 10, 3, 1, 8, 5, 2, 9, 4, 6)
    u = ''.join(s[i] for i in perm)

    v = ''.join(
        chr(32 + ((ord(ch) - 32 + 17 * i) % 95))
        for i, ch in enumerate(u)
    )

    return hashlib.sha256(v.encode("utf-8")).hexdigest() == (
        "b94d27b9934d3e08a52e52d7da7dabfac484efe37a5380ee9088f7ace2efcde9"
    )
```

```python

def mystery(x):
    if not isinstance(x, str):
        return False
    x = x.strip()
    if not x or x[0] == '0':
        return False
    try:
        n = int(x)
    except ValueError:
        return False
    if n <= 0:
        return False

    def is_prime(p):
        if p < 2: return False
        if p == 2: return True
        if p % 2 == 0: return False
        for i in range(3, int(p**0.5) + 1, 2):
            if p % i == 0: return False
        return True

    # Find integer cube root of n
    c = int(round(n ** (1.0 / 3)))
    found = False
    for delta in range(-2, 3):
        if (c + delta) ** 3 == n:
            c = c + delta
            found = True
            break
    if not found:
        return False

    digit_sum = sum(int(d) for d in str(n))
    b = bin(c)[2:]  # binary representation of c (no '0b' prefix)

    return (
        is_prime(digit_sum) and
        is_prime(c) and
        is_prime(c * c + c + 1) and
        b == b[::-1]
    )

```
### Explanation


This puzzle requires finding a string representation of a positive integer `n` satisfying **four simultaneous conditions** on its integer cube root `c = ∛n`:

1. `n` must be a **perfect cube** (with integer cube root `c`)
2. The **digit sum** of `n` must be **prime**
3. `c` itself must be **prime**
4. The value `c² + c + 1` must be **prime**
5. The **binary representation** of `c` must be a **palindrome**

These constraints interact in a subtle and non-obvious way, making the solution hard to guess without systematic exploration.



### Outcome
Solved

*** inventor claude-sonnet-4-6
```python
def mystery(x):
    if isinstance(x, bool):
        return False
    if isinstance(x, (int, float)):
        s = str(x)
    elif isinstance(x, str):
        s = x
    else:
        return False

    s = s.strip()
    if len(s) != 9 or not s.isdigit() or s[0] == '0':
        return False

    d = [int(c) for c in s]

    perm = tuple((2 * i + 3) % 9 for i in range(9))
    a = sum((i + 1) * d[perm[i]] for i in range(9))

    b = 0
    for i, v in enumerate(d):
        b = (b * 13 + (v ^ (i + 4))) % 1000

    c = 0
    for v in reversed(d):
        c = (c * 7 + v) % 97

    p = 1
    for v in d:
        p = (p * v) % 97

    return a == 159 and b == 137 and c == 77 and p == 57 and sum(d) == 39
```

This puzzle takes a 9-digit integer (as int or string) and puts it through four cryptographic-style hash-like constraint checks plus a digit-sum check:

1. **Permuted weighted sum** (`a`): digits are reordered via a specific permutation `(3,5,7,0,2,4,6,8,1)`, then weighted by position index — must equal 159.
2. **Sequential XOR-hash** (`b`): iterative computation mixing multiplication by 13, XOR with `(i+4)`, and mod 1000 — must equal 137.
3. **Reverse sequential hash** (`c`): digits processed in reverse, iteratively via `c = (c*7 + v) % 97` — must equal 77.
4. **Product hash** (`p`): running product of digits mod 97 — must equal 57.
5. **Digit sum** must equal 39.

Each constraint is easy to verify but hard to invert, and all five together are designed to pinpoint essentially a unique solution.

SOLUTION: 271828182

# Rules
We take turns. One player, the proposer, gives the other a code puzzle with a Python function called `mystery` that takes a value x (either string or number) and returns a boolean. The other player, the solver, have to find a value x for which the function returns True. The proposer's job is to make the puzzle as hard as they can, i.e. it should be very difficult to find out which values the function returns True for. To prove that the puzzle is solvable, the proposer must give a sample solution. If the sample solution turns out to be wrong, the proposer is penalized.

```python
        return False
```

```python
def mystery(x):
    if isinstance(x, str):
        if len(x) != 9 or not x.isdigit() or x[0] == "0":
            return False
        s = x
    elif type(x) is int and 100000000 <= x <= 999999999:
        s = str(x)
    else:
        return False

    d = [ord(c) - 48 for c in s]
    perm = (2, 5, 1, 8, 0, 3, 7, 4, 6)

    a = sum((i + 1) * d[perm[i]] for i in range(9))

    b = 0
    for i, v in enumerate(d):
        b = (b * 13 + (v ^ (i + 4))) % 1000

    c = 0
    for v in reversed(d):
        c = (c * 7 + v) % 97

    p = 1
    for v in d:
        p = (p * v) % 97

    return a == 238 and b == 948 and c == 17 and p == 2 and sum(d) == 42

```

### Code and Explanation
```python

def mystery(x):
    if not isinstance(x, str):
        return False
    x = x.strip()
    if not x or x[0] == '0':
        return False
    try:
        n = int(x)
    except ValueError:
        return False
    if n <= 0:
        return False

    def is_prime(p):
        if p < 2: return False
        if p == 2: return True
        if p % 2 == 0: return False
        for i in range(3, int(p**0.5) + 1, 2):
            if p % i == 0: return False
        return True

    # Find integer cube root of n
    c = int(round(n ** (1.0 / 3)))
    found = False
    for delta in range(-2, 3):
        if (c + delta) ** 3 == n:
            c = c + delta
            found = True
            break
    if not found:
        return False

    digit_sum = sum(int(d) for d in str(n))
    b = bin(c)[2:]  # binary representation of c (no '0b' prefix)

    return (
        is_prime(digit_sum) and
        is_prime(c) and
        is_prime(c * c + c + 1) and
        b == b[::-1]
    )

```
### Explanation


This puzzle requires finding a string representation of a positive integer `n` satisfying **four simultaneous conditions** on its integer cube root `c = ∛n`:

1. `n` must be a **perfect cube** (with integer cube root `c`)
2. The **digit sum** of `n` must be **prime**
3. `c` itself must be **prime**
4. The value `c² + c + 1` must be **prime**
5. The **binary representation** of `c` must be a **palindrome**

These constraints interact in a subtle and non-obvious way, making the solution hard to guess without systematic exploration.



### Outcome
Solved

```python
import hashlib

def mystery(x):
    if isinstance(x, bool):
        return False
    if isinstance(x, (int, float)):
        s = str(x)
    elif isinstance(x, str):
        s = x
    else:
        return False

    s = s.strip()
    if len(s) != 11 or not all(32 <= ord(c) <= 126 for c in s):
        return False

    perm = (4, 2, 0, 10, 7, 1, 9, 6, 3, 8, 5)
    u = ''.join(s[i] for i in perm)
    scrambled = bytes((ord(ch) ^ (0x41 + i)) for i, ch in enumerate(u))
    z = scrambled[::-1].decode("latin-1")

    return hashlib.sha256(z.encode("utf-8")).hexdigest() == (
        "b94d27b9934d3e08a52e52d7da7dabfac484efe37a5380ee9088f7ace2efcde9"
    )

```

### Code and Explanation
```python

import math

def mystery(x):
    if not isinstance(x, int) or isinstance(x, bool):
        return False
    if x < 10000 or x > 99999:
        return False

    # x must be a perfect square
    r = math.isqrt(x)
    if r * r != x:
        return False

    # All five digits must be nonzero and distinct
    digits = [int(d) for d in str(x)]
    if 0 in digits or len(set(digits)) != 5:
        return False

    # Digit sum must be prime
    s = sum(digits)
    if s < 2 or not all(s % i != 0 for i in range(2, s)):
        return False

    # Combined modular constraint
    if x % 77 != 56:
        return False

    return True

```
### Explanation


**Explanation (private):**

The puzzle asks for a 5-digit perfect square satisfying four conditions simultaneously:
1. It's a perfect square
2. All five decimal digits are distinct and nonzero
3. The digit sum is prime
4. `x ≡ 56 (mod 77)` — by CRT this encodes `x ≡ 0 (mod 7)` AND `x ≡ 1 (mod 11)`, which forces the square root `a` to satisfy `a ≡ 0 (mod 7)` AND `a ≡ ±1 (mod 11)`

Exhaustive analysis of the 5-digit perfect squares (100² through 316²) satisfying `a ≡ 21 or 56 (mod 77)` shows that only **a = 133**, **x = 17689** passes all four conditions: digits {1,7,6,8,9} are all distinct and nonzero, digit sum = 31 is prime, and 17689 = 77×229 + 56.



### Outcome

```python
import hashlib

def mystery(x):
    if isinstance(x, bool):
        return False
    if isinstance(x, (int, float)):
        s = str(x)
    elif isinstance(x, str):
        s = x
    else:
        return False

    s = s.strip()
    if len(s) != 11 or not all(32 <= ord(c) <= 126 for c in s):
        return False

    perm = (7, 0, 10, 3, 1, 8, 5, 2, 9, 4, 6)
    u = ''.join(s[i] for i in perm)

    v = ''.join(
        chr(32 + ((ord(ch) - 32 + 17 * i) % 95))
        for i, ch in enumerate(u)
    )

    return hashlib.sha256(v.encode("utf-8")).hexdigest() == (
        "b94d27b9934d3e08a52e52d7da7dabfac484efe37a5380ee9088f7ace2efcde9"
    )

```

```python
def mystery(x):
    if isinstance(x, bool):
        return False
    if isinstance(x, (int, float)):
        s = str(x)
    elif isinstance(x, str):
        s = x
    else:
        return False

    s = s.strip()
    if len(s) != 9 or not s.isdigit() or s[0] == '0':
        return False

    d = [int(c) for c in s]

    perm = (3, 6, 0, 7, 4, 1, 8, 5, 2)
    a = sum((i + 1) * d[perm[i]] for i in range(9))

    b = 0
    for i, v in enumerate(d):
        b = (b * 13 + (v ^ (i + 4))) % 1000

    c = 0
    for v in reversed(d):
        c = (c * 7 + v) % 97

    p = 1
    for v in d:
        p = (p * v) % 97

    q = sum(d[i] * (i * i + 1) for i in range(9)) % 101

    return a == 207 and b == 217 and c == 43 and p == 3 and q == 50
```

This puzzle takes a 9-digit number and applies five independent constraints:

1. **`a`**: A permutation-scrambled weighted sum — the digits are reordered by `perm` before being multiplied by position weights `1..9`
2. **`b`**: A rolling hash (multiplier 13, mod 1000) where each digit is XOR'd with its index offset before accumulation
3. **`c`**: A reverse-order rolling hash (multiplier 7, mod 97) over the digits
4. **`p`**: The product of all digits reduced mod 97
5. **`q`**: A quadratic-weighted digit sum reduced mod 101

Each constraint involves a different nonlinear combination of the digits, making it hard to jointly satisfy all five without knowing the target value.

SOLUTION: 726384951

Let's play a game.

# Rules
We take turns. One player, the proposer, gives the other a code puzzle with a Python function called `mystery` that takes a value x (either string or number) and returns a boolean. The other player, the solver, have to find a value x for which the function returns True. The proposer's job is to make the puzzle as hard as they can, i.e. it should be very difficult to find out which values the function returns True for. To prove that the puzzle is solvable, the proposer must give a sample solution. If the sample solution turns out to be wrong, the proposer is penalized.

# Instructions
You are the proposer. I am the solver.

Respond with a Python code block first, then explain your puzzle. Everything outside the code block is private -- I cannot see it.
The last line of your response should contain only: `SOLUTION: x` where x is the value of a sample solution for your puzzle. Ensure that `mystery(x)` is valid Python code given your x. For example, it is very important to use quotes if you believe `x` is a string.

Please make sure that your sample solution works -- you will be penalized if not. Do not give multiple code blocks or puzzles.

# History
Here are some puzzles from past turns of the game. You should NOT repeat them, but you can learn from them to ensure your puzzle is difficult enough.

## Puzzle

### Proposer
This is MY puzzle.

### Code and Explanation
```python

import hashlib

_TABLE = str.maketrans(
    "abcdefghijklmnopqrstuvwxyz",
    "phqgiumeaylnofdxjkrcvstzwb"
)

def mystery(x):
    if not isinstance(x, str):
        return False
    y = x.strip().casefold().translate(_TABLE)[::-1]
    return hashlib.sha256(y.encode("utf-8")).hexdigest() == (
        "ba7816bf8f01cfea414140de5dae2223b00361a396177a9cb410ff61f20015ad"
    )

```
### Explanation
(Hidden to you, since this is my puzzle.)

### Outcome
Solved

### Code and Explanation
```python

def mystery(x):
    if not isinstance(x, str):
        return False
    if len(x) != 5 or not x.isalpha() or not x.islower():
        return False
    p = [ord(c) - 96 for c in x]  # a=1, b=2, ..., z=26
    return (
        p[0] ** 2 - p[1] ** 2 == 168 and
        p[0] ** 2 - p[3] ** 2 == 25 and
        p[2] ** 2 + p[4] ** 2 == 281 and
```

### Code and Explanation
```python

def mystery(x):
    if not isinstance(x, str):
        return False
    x = x.strip()
    if not x or x[0] == '0':
        return False
    try:
        n = int(x)
    except ValueError:
        return False
    if n <= 0:
        return False

    def is_prime(p):
        if p < 2: return False
        if p == 2: return True
        if p % 2 == 0: return False
        for i in range(3, int(p**0.5) + 1, 2):
            if p % i == 0: return False
        return True

    # Find integer cube root of n
    c = int(round(n ** (1.0 / 3)))
    found = False
    for delta in range(-2, 3):
        if (c + delta) ** 3 == n:
            c = c + delta
            found = True
            break
    if not found:
        return False

    digit_sum = sum(int(d) for d in str(n))
    b = bin(c)[2:]  # binary representation of c (no '0b' prefix)

    return (
        is_prime(digit_sum) and
        is_prime(c) and
        is_prime(c * c + c + 1) and
        b == b[::-1]
    )

```
### Explanation


This puzzle requires finding a string representation of a positive integer `n` satisfying **four simultaneous conditions** on its integer cube root `c = ∛n`:

1. `n` must be a **perfect cube** (with integer cube root `c`)
2. The **digit sum** of `n` must be **prime**
3. `c` itself must be **prime**
4. The value `c² + c + 1` must be **prime**
5. The **binary representation** of `c` must be a **palindrome**

These constraints interact in a subtle and non-obvious way, making the solution hard to guess without systematic exploration.

```python
import hashlib

def mystery(x):
    if isinstance(x, bool):
        return False
    if isinstance(x, (int, float)):
        s = str(x)
    elif isinstance(x, str):
        s = x
    else:
        return False

    s = s.strip()
    if len(s) != 11 or not all(32 <= ord(c) <= 126 for c in s):
        return False

    perm = (4, 2, 0, 10, 7, 1, 9, 6, 3, 8, 5)
    u = ''.join(s[i] for i in perm)
    scrambled = bytes((ord(ch) ^ (0x41 + i)) for i, ch in enumerate(u))
    z = scrambled[::-1].decode("latin-1")

    return hashlib.sha256(z.encode("utf-8")).hexdigest() == (
        "b94d27b9934d3e08a52e52d7da7dabfac484efe37a5380ee9088f7ace2efcde9"
    )
```

### Code and Explanation
```python

import math

def mystery(x):
    if not isinstance(x, int) or isinstance(x, bool):
        return False
    if x < 10000 or x > 99999:
        return False

    # x must be a perfect square
    r = math.isqrt(x)
    if r * r != x:
        return False

    # All five digits must be nonzero and distinct
    digits = [int(d) for d in str(x)]
    if 0 in digits or len(set(digits)) != 5:
        return False

    # Digit sum must be prime
    s = sum(digits)
    if s < 2 or not all(s % i != 0 for i in range(2, s)):
        return False

    # Combined modular constraint
    if x % 77 != 56:
        return False

    return True

```
### Explanation


**Explanation (private):**

The puzzle asks for a 5-digit perfect square satisfying four conditions simultaneously:
1. It's a perfect square
2. All five decimal digits are distinct and nonzero
3. The digit sum is prime
4. `x ≡ 56 (mod 77)` — by CRT this encodes `x ≡ 0 (mod 7)` AND `x ≡ 1 (mod 11)`, which forces the square root `a` to satisfy `a ≡ 0 (mod 7)` AND `a ≡ ±1 (mod 11)`

Exhaustive analysis of the 5-digit perfect squares (100² through 316²) satisfying `a ≡ 21 or 56 (mod 77)` shows that only **a = 133**, **x = 17689** passes all four conditions: digits {1,7,6,8,9} are all distinct and nonzero, digit sum = 31 is prime, and 17689 = 77×229 + 56.

```python
import math

def mystery(x):
    if not isinstance(x, int) or isinstance(x, bool):
        return False
    if x < 100000 or x > 999999:
        return False

    digits = [int(d) for d in str(x)]

    # All six digits must be distinct
    if len(set(digits)) != 6:
        return False

    # x must be a perfect square
    r2 = math.isqrt(x)
    if r2 * r2 != x:
        return False

    # The square root must also be a perfect square
    r4 = math.isqrt(r2)
    if r4 * r4 != r2:
        return False

    # The digit sum must equal the 4th root of x
    if sum(digits) != r4:
        return False

    return True
```

**Explanation of the puzzle (visible to you):**

Find a 6-digit positive integer `x` satisfying four simultaneous constraints:

1. **All six decimal digits are distinct** — no repeated digit.
2. **x is a perfect square** — i.e., `x = r²` for some integer `r`.
3. **That square root is itself a perfect square** — i.e., `r = s²`, meaning `x = s⁴` is a perfect *fourth power*.
4. **Self-referential digit constraint** — the sum of the digits of `x` equals `s`, the fourth root of `x`.

The beauty is in constraint 4: the number "tells you" its own fourth root through its digit sum. There turns out to be a unique 6-digit integer satisfying all four constraints simultaneously (two near-miss candidates are eliminated by constraint 1).

SOLUTION: 390625

f(x) = reverse(xor_key(permute(x)))

Where:
- permute(x) applies a permutation to the characters of x
- xor_key(y) XORs each character with a key
- reverse() reverses the string

The inverse is:
x = inverse_permute(xor_key(reverse(f(x))))

So if I want f(x) = "abc" (3 chars), I need x to be 3 chars.

But I want x to be 10 chars, and f(x) to produce a 10-char string that gets hashed.

Actually, the transformation f(x) → SHA256(f(x)) is not invertible (SHA256 is one-way). So I need to pick x, compute f(x), compute SHA256(f(x)), and include SHA256(f(x)) in the code.

The problem is I can't compute SHA256 in my head. But what if I design f to produce a string whose SHA256 I already know? I could make f the identity function for a specific input, so sha256(f(x)) equals a hash I've precomputed.

Actually, let me simplify this. What if I use something like ROT13 as the transformation? Then the puzzle becomes: find x such that ROT13(x) hashes to a known value. The solver would need to figure out that the answer is the ROT13 of some target string, but they only see the hash. For this to work as a puzzle, I need to think about what makes it solvable without brute force.

For short strings (3 characters), brute force is feasible with 26^3 = 17,576 possibilities. But for a 10-character string with 95 printable ASCII characters, there are roughly 6×10^19 possibilities—completely infeasible. However, if I add constraints like "lowercase letters only" or "4 digits followed by 6 letters," the search space becomes small enough to brute force again.

So my approach is to pick a transformation f, choose a long enough x to prevent brute force, compute f(x) and its SHA256 hash, and include that hash in the code.

These constraints make the puzzle feel structured while keeping the search space large enough to prevent brute force. at most 9×6=54).

Continuing through higher values like 8, 9, and beyond, none of them produce a 4th power whose digits sum back to k.

```python
_TABLE = str.maketrans(
```

```python
def mystery(x):
    if isinstance(x, str):
        if len(x) != 9 or not x.isdigit() or x[0] == "0":
            return False
        s = x
    elif type(x) is int and 100000000 <= x <= 999999999:
        s = str(x)
    else:
        return False

    d = [ord(c) - 48 for c in s]
    perm = (2, 5, 1, 8, 0, 3, 7, 4, 6)

    a = sum((i + 1) * d[perm[i]] for i in range(9))

    b = 0
    for i, v in enumerate(d):
        b = (b * 13 + (v ^ (i + 4))) % 1000

    c = 0
    for v in reversed(d):
        c = (c * 7 + v) % 97

    p = 1
    for v in d:
        p = (p * v) % 97

    return a == 238 and b == 948 and c == 17 and p == 2 and sum(d) == 42

```
### Explanation


This one is a checksum-style puzzle over a 9-digit decimal value. It mixes a digit permutation, a rolling XOR-scrambled residue, a reverse-order base-7 fold, and a modular product check, so it’s not obvious by inspection which inputs satisfy all the conditions.

### Code and Explanation
```python

def mystery(x):
    if not isinstance(x, str):
        return False
    x = x.strip()
    if not x or x[0] == '0':
        return False
    try:
        n = int(x)
    except ValueError:
        return False
    if n <= 0:
        return False

    def is_prime(p):
        if p < 2: return False
        if p == 2: return True
        if p % 2 == 0: return False
        for i in range(3, int(p**0.5) + 1, 2):
            if p % i == 0: return False
        return True

    # Find integer cube root of n
    c = int(round(n ** (1.0 / 3)))
    found = False
    for delta in range(-2, 3):
        if (c + delta) ** 3 == n:
            c = c + delta
            found = True
            break
    if not found:
        return False

    digit_sum = sum(int(d) for d in str(n))
    b = bin(c)[2:]  # binary representation of c (no '0b' prefix)

    return (
        is_prime(digit_sum) and
        is_prime(c) and
        is_prime(c * c + c + 1) and
        b == b[::-1]
    )

```
### Explanation
(Hidden to you, since this is my puzzle.)

```python

import hashlib

def mystery(x):
    if isinstance(x, bool):
        return False
    if isinstance(x, (int, float)):
        s = str(x)
    elif isinstance(x, str):
        s = x
    else:
        return False

    s = s.strip()
    if len(s) != 11 or not all(32 <= ord(c) <= 126 for c in s):
        return False

    perm = (4, 2, 0, 10, 7, 1, 9, 6, 3, 8, 5)
    u = ''.join(s[i] for i in perm)
    scrambled = bytes((ord(ch) ^ (0x41 + i)) for i, ch in enumerate(u))
    z = scrambled[::-1].decode("latin-1")

    return hashlib.sha256(z.encode("utf-8")).hexdigest() == (
        "b94d27b9934d3e08a52e52d7da7dabfac484efe37a5380ee9088f7ace2efcde9"
    )

```
### Explanation


The input is first normalized, then its characters are permuted, XOR-scrambled with a position-dependent mask, reversed, and finally hashed. The returned value is True only when that transformed text matches a fixed SHA-256 digest.



### Outcome

```python

import math

def mystery(x):
    if not isinstance(x, int) or isinstance(x, bool):
        return False
    if x < 10000 or x > 99999:
        return False

    # x must be a perfect square
    r = math.isqrt(x)
    if r * r != x:
        return False

    # All five digits must be nonzero and distinct
    digits = [int(d) for d in str(x)]
    if 0 in digits or len(set(digits)) != 5:
        return False

    # Digit sum must be prime
    s = sum(digits)
    if s < 2 or not all(s % i != 0 for i in range(2, s)):
        return False

    # Combined modular constraint
    if x % 77 != 56:
        return False

    return True

```

```python
import hashlib

def mystery(x):

        return False
```

```python

import math

def mystery(x):
    if not isinstance(x, int) or isinstance(x, bool):
        return False
    if x < 100000 or x > 999999:
        return False

    digits = [int(d) for d in str(x)]

    # All six digits must be distinct
    if len(set(digits)) != 6:
        return False

    # x must be a perfect square
    r2 = math.isqrt(x)
    if r2 * r2 != x:
        return False

    # The square root must also be a perfect square
    r4 = math.isqrt(r2)
    if r4 * r4 != r2:
        return False

    # The digit sum must equal the 4th root of x
    if sum(digits) != r4:
        return False

    return True

```

```python
import hashlib

def mystery(x):
    if not isinstance(x, str):
        return False
    if len(x) != 43:
        return False
    if not all(33 <= ord(c) <= 126 for c in x):
        return False

    y = ''.join(chr(32 + ((ord(c) - 33) % 95)) for c in x[::-1])
    return hashlib.sha1(y.encode("ascii")).hexdigest() == (
        "2fd4e1c67a2d28fced849ee1bb76e7391b93eb12"
    )
```

This puzzle reverses the string, shifts every character one step backward in the printable ASCII range, and then checks the SHA-1 hash of the result against a fixed digest. The digest is for a well-known pangram, but the reverse-and-shift layer hides the actual input pretty effectively.

SOLUTION: "hpe!z{bm!fiu!sfwp!tqnvk!ypg!oxpsc!ldjvr!fiU"

## Analysis

Let me work backwards from the requirements:

1. `x` must be a 43-character string with ASCII values 33-126
2. `y` is formed by reversing `x` and applying: `chr(32 + ((ord(c) - 33) % 95))` to each character
3. `y`'s SHA1 must equal `"2fd4e1c67a2d28fced849ee1bb76e7391b93eb12"`

**Step 1:** Recognize the SHA1 hash. `2fd4e1c67a2d28fced849ee1bb76e7391b93eb12` is the well-known SHA1 of `"The quick brown fox jumps over the lazy dog"` (43 characters ✓).

So `y = "The quick brown fox jumps over the lazy dog"`.

**Step 2:** Invert the transformation. For input `c` with `33 ≤ ord(c) ≤ 126`:
- `(ord(c) - 33)` is in `[0, 93]`, so `% 95` has no effect
- The transformation simplifies to: `output = chr(ord(c) - 1)`
- Therefore the **inverse** is: `input = chr(ord(y_char) + 1)`

**Step 3:** Compute `x_rev` (= reversed `x`) by adding 1 to each character's ASCII value in `y`:
- Spaces (`32`) → `!` (`33`) ✓ (in valid range)
- `z` (`122`) → `{` (`123`) ✓ (in valid range)
- Result: `"Uif!rvjdl!cspxo!gpy!kvnqt!pwfs!uif!mb{z!eph"`

**Step 4:** Reverse to get `x`:
`"hpe!z{bm!fiu!sfwp!tqnvk!ypg!oxpsc!ldvjr!fiU"`

**Verification:**
```python
x = "hpe!z{bm!fiu!sfwp!tqnvk!ypg!oxpsc!ldvjr!fiU"
# len = 43 ✓
# all ASCII 33-126 ✓
# x[::-1] = "Uif!rvjdl!cspxo!gpy!kvnqt!pwfs!uif!mb{z!eph"
# transform → "The quick brown fox jumps over the lazy dog"
# SHA1 = 2fd4e1c67a2d28fced849ee1bb76e7391b93eb12 ✓
```

SOLUTION: "hpe!z{bm!fiu!sfwp!tqnvk!ypg!oxpsc!ldvjr!fiU"

```python

def mystery(x):
    if not isinstance(x, str):
        return False
    x = x.strip()
    if not x or x[0] == '0':
        return False
    try:
        n = int(x)
    except ValueError:
        return False
    if n <= 0:
        return False

    def is_prime(p):
        if p < 2: return False
        if p == 2: return True
        if p % 2 == 0: return False
        for i in range(3, int(p**0.5) + 1, 2):
            if p % i == 0: return False
        return True

    # Find integer cube root of n
    c = int(round(n ** (1.0 / 3)))
    found = False
    for delta in range(-2, 3):
        if (c + delta) ** 3 == n:
            c = c + delta
            found = True
            break
    if not found:
        return False

    digit_sum = sum(int(d) for d in str(n))
    b = bin(c)[2:]  # binary representation of c (no '0b' prefix)

    return (
        is_prime(digit_sum) and
        is_prime(c) and
        is_prime(c * c + c + 1) and

    )

```

```python
import hashlib

def mystery(x):
    if isinstance(x, bool):
        return False
    if isinstance(x, (int, float)):
        s = str(x)
    elif isinstance(x, str):
        s = x
    else:
        return False

    s = s.strip()
    if len(s) != 11 or not all(32 <= ord(c) <= 126 for c in s):
        return False

    perm = (4, 2, 0, 10, 7, 1, 9, 6, 3, 8, 5)
    u = ''.join(s[i] for i in perm)
    scrambled = bytes((ord(ch) ^ (0x41 + i)) for i, ch in enumerate(u))
    z = scrambled[::-1].decode("latin-1")

    return hashlib.sha256(z.encode("utf-8")).hexdigest() == (
        "b94d27b9934d3e08a52e52d7da7dabfac484efe37a5380ee9088f7ace2efcde9"
    )
```

```python

import math

def mystery(x):
    if not isinstance(x, int) or isinstance(x, bool):
        return False
    if x < 10000 or x > 99999:
        return False

    # x must be a perfect square
    r = math.isqrt(x)
    if r * r != x:
        return False

    # All five digits must be nonzero and distinct
    digits = [int(d) for d in str(x)]
    if 0 in digits or len(set(digits)) != 5:
        return False

    # Digit sum must be prime
    s = sum(digits)
    if s < 2 or not all(s % i != 0 for i in range(2, s)):
        return False

    # Combined modular constraint
    if x % 77 != 56:
        return False

    return True

```
### Explanation


**Explanation (private):**

The puzzle asks for a 5-digit perfect square satisfying four conditions simultaneously:
1. It's a perfect square
2. All five decimal digits are distinct and nonzero
3. The digit sum is prime
4. `x ≡ 56 (mod 77)` — by CRT this encodes `x ≡ 0 (mod 7)` AND `x ≡ 1 (mod 11)`, which forces the square root `a` to satisfy `a ≡ 0 (mod 7)` AND `a ≡ ±1 (mod 11)`

Exhaustive analysis of the 5-digit perfect squares (100² through 316²) satisfying `a ≡ 21 or 56 (mod 77)` shows that only **a = 133**, **x = 17689** passes all four conditions: digits {1,7,6,8,9} are all distinct and nonzero, digit sum = 31 is prime, and 17689 = 77×229 + 56.

### Code and Explanation
```python

import math

def mystery(x):
    if not isinstance(x, int) or isinstance(x, bool):
        return False
    if x < 100000 or x > 999999:
        return False

    digits = [int(d) for d in str(x)]

    # All six digits must be distinct
    if len(set(digits)) != 6:
        return False

    # x must be a perfect square
    r2 = math.isqrt(x)
    if r2 * r2 != x:
        return False

    # The square root must also be a perfect square
    r4 = math.isqrt(r2)
    if r4 * r4 != r2:
        return False

    # The digit sum must equal the 4th root of x
    if sum(digits) != r4:
        return False

    return True

```
### Explanation


**Explanation of the puzzle (visible to you):**

Find a 6-digit positive integer `x` satisfying four simultaneous constraints:

1. **All six decimal digits are distinct** — no repeated digit.
2. **x is a perfect square** — i.e., `x = r²` for some integer `r`.
3. **That square root is itself a perfect square** — i.e., `r = s²`, meaning `x = s⁴` is a perfect *fourth power*.
4. **Self-referential digit constraint** — the sum of the digits of `x` equals `s`, the fourth root of `x`.

The beauty is in constraint 4: the number "tells you" its own fourth root through its digit sum. There turns out to be a unique 6-digit integer satisfying all four constraints simultaneously (two near-miss candidates are eliminated by constraint 1).



### Outcome
Solved

```python
import math

def mystery(x):
    if not isinstance(x, int) or isinstance(x, bool):
        return False
    if x < 1000 or x > 9999:
        return False

    # All four decimal digits must be distinct
    s = str(x)
    if len(set(s)) != 4:
        return False

    # x must be a perfect square
    r = math.isqrt(x)
    if r * r != x:
        return False

    # The square root must itself be prime
    def is_prime(n):
        if n < 2:
            return False
        if n == 2:
            return True
        if n % 2 == 0:
            return False
        for i in range(3, int(n**0.5) + 1, 2):
            if n % i == 0:
                return False
        return True

    if not is_prime(r):
        return False

    # x must equal k! + 1 for some positive integer k
    k, factorial = 1, 1
    while factorial < x:
        k += 1
        factorial *= k
        if factorial + 1 == x:
            return True

    return False
```

This puzzle asks you to find a 4-digit integer satisfying **four simultaneous constraints**:

1. All four decimal digits are **distinct**
2. It is a **perfect square**
3. Its square root is **prime**
4. It equals **k! + 1** for some positive integer k (one more than a factorial)

The intersection of factorial numbers, perfect squares, and primality constraints is very tight — you'll need to find the right k where these all align at once in the 4-digit range.
